    <li> First point of note: this, the Colonel Conk, and a half-dozen others like this are all the same model. There&#x27;s no point paying more for one than another other.    The finish is a nice chrome. Doesn&#x27;t seem to scratch easily. Looks nice on the counter, fairly classy with a Merkur razor and Escali brush. Nothing falls out. No complaints about usability.    The problem, though, is that the bottom is a stick-on piece of foam that compresses after a week or two. That brings the metal base level with the counter. If you splash water anywhere near it, the base will begin to rust.    The solution is to pull off and discard the foam, exposing a lead (?) disk that weights the base. Then you stick a couple of rubber bumpers (the kind you&#x27;d attach to the inside of cabinet doors) to the disk to elevate the assembly a few millimeters higher. (I also drilled a hole in a larger fabric bumper and mounted it above the disk to push the disk down.)    The short elevation actually makes the thing look better. Hasn&#x27;t rusted at all in two months.</li>

    <li> This is a really great looking, shiny stand.  It doesn&#x27;t look cheap at all.  The only issue I had was that the stand wouldn&#x27;t hold the Parker silvertip brush I purchased by its handle as it was about a 1/16th of an inch too small.  You could however hold it by the brushes knot which works pretty well, but causes the brush to tip over.  I got tired of the balancing act, and I was able to spread the holders out enough to comfortably hold the brush without damaging the stand.  I am not recommending you try this, but it did work for me.  I had a hair brush that had a slightly oval handle that would slide into the holder on its skinny side but not the larger side.  I put the handle between the brush holder on the skinny side of the brush and turned the brush basically forcing the stand holder &amp;#34;tines&amp;#34; outward about 1/8th of an inch. This game me just enough room and you can&#x27;t even tell that the tines were bent.  If you try this, know that you may damage the stand and void any warranty.</li>

The finish is high quality, the base is weighted so that the stand has the stability that you would expect. Also note that the shaft is knurled for a sure grip and the base has a non-slip rubber-like pad to prevent slippage. My only cautionary note is that some shaving brush handles may be too wide for this (or for most shaving brush stands). The part of your shaving brush that you will use to hang from this stand should be narrower than 1&amp;#34;. This limitation is relatively standard for shaving brush stands so if your brush is thicker, you likely will need to pass on using a stand to dry your brush. Overall, I highly recommend this stand and I would buy it again or give it to a friend without reservation.</li>

    <li> The price of shaving has gotten ridiculous, so I looked into the alternatives. I learned on the safety razor, so had no particular fear of it. My total investment was about $80.  The safety razor is not the rickety trap door type and I don&#x27;t foresee needing replacement ever.  Not sure how long I can expect the $10 brush to last, but if I live long enough, I believe the savings in blades will show a profit over \\&quot;modern\\&quot; shaving.  Not a significant amount, that&#x27;s why I mentioned the driving the extra miles to at least not hating the drive. The satisfaction is a little like if I had found a way to extend one gallon of gas into 3 and drive a larger, better car for less in the long run than my little 4 cylinder.  The shave is better by far. Expect a few scrapes early on, but it gets better in time.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Oh, the stand. It is a lovely, I mean masculine, addition to my vanity. Well weighted, with the substantial razor and the brush on I have not come close to knocking it over. The prongs are bent upward slightly to keep from too easily knocking  the razor and brush off accidently.  It can still be done, but has not quite happened to me yet. It needs to be wiped dry almost every use to keep it looking sexy because water gets on it and will leave water marks, easily removed from the highly polished finish.  I suspect it will also prevent corrosion, I suspect.  It&#x27;s not quite \\&quot;universal\\&quot;. I enjoyed my experience so much that I purchased a shaving \\&quot;set\\&quot; as a gift  that included this stand.     <li> I&#x27;ve had this thing for a month and it reminds me of chrome plated brass fixtures from houses built 100 years ago.  I was just prompted for a review and so I took it apart.  Inside the base is a cast iron weight with a high quality foam pad glued to the bottom.  Over that goes a stamped (probably chrome plated brass) metal cover that is thickly chrome plated completely inside and out.  Even the inside you would never normally see is plated.  There is a 1\\&quot; piece of all-thread that screws into the cast iron base, through the stamped metal cover into the upright shaft.  There is another 1\\&quot; piece of all-thread which screws into the top of the shaft, through the flange that holds the razor and brush, and into a acorn nut--the top of the unit.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It&#x27;s made just like you would think, just like it would have been 100 years ago and for the most part out of quality materials that would have been used 100 years ago.  I&#x27;ve tested all pieces with a magnet and the only parts that are not non-metallic are the cast iron base weight (which is necessary and a good design feature) and the two short pieces of all-thread that fit into either end of the shaft to hold it all together--not a good design feature.  They appear to be plated to look like brass, but they are definitely magnetic.  I would think it would be easy to make this product with brass all-thread, but I still give it 5 stars.  With some spit and polish, this product will have value in 50 or 100 years.  This is not Wal-Mart/3rd World crap.</li>
